<div id="review_601189666" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/601189666" />
    <a title="Szplug" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1843184-szplug"><img alt="Szplug" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1395686229p2/1843184.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/601189666?book_show_action=true">Apr 27, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Szplug" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Szplug" href="/user/show/1843184-szplug">Szplug</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780679724650" href="/book/show/529326.Beyond_Good_and_Evil">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer601189666" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14409205158512784971">As with my review of <i>Thus Spoke Zarathustra</i>, the below comprises the notes I jotted down—deciphered as best could be managed against the near hieroglyphic obfuscation of the chicken riot I call handwriting—when this was read some dozen or so years ago. As I failed to consistently make clear what were Nietzsche's words, as set against my own thoughts on the latter, the non-italicized portions may represent one giant act of plagiarizing. Luckily for me, the man seldom presented himself as possessi</span>
  <span id="freeText14409205158512784971" style="display:none">As with my review of <i>Thus Spoke Zarathustra</i>, the below comprises the notes I jotted down—deciphered as best could be managed against the near hieroglyphic obfuscation of the chicken riot I call handwriting—when this was read some dozen or so years ago. As I failed to consistently make clear what were Nietzsche's words, as set against my own thoughts on the latter, the non-italicized portions may represent one giant act of plagiarizing. Luckily for me, the man seldom presented himself as possessing a litigious personality.<br /><blockquote>
  <i>Gradually it has become clear to me what every great philosophy so far has been: namely, the personal confession of its author and a kind of involuntary and unconscious memoir; also that the moral (or immoral) intentions in every philosophy constituted the real germ of life from which the whole plant had grown.<br /><br />The free and unfree wills are mythology—in real life it is only a matter of strong and weak wills.</i>
</blockquote>We manipulate our prejudices and desires with language to make our symbols and interpretations <i>be</i> reality—this is our inscribed presumption.<br /><blockquote>
  <i>Our highest insights must—and should—sound like follies and sometimes like crimes when they are heard without permission by those who are not predisposed and predestined for them.</i>
</blockquote>Why does there have to be a <i>truth</i> and <i>falsity</i> in opposition? Why not gradations of values? Why can't the world that concerns us be a fiction and why does it need an author?<br /><blockquote>
  <i>There is something about truth, about the search for truth; and when a human being is too human about it—he seeks to do only the good—I bet he finds nothing.</i>
</blockquote>Has the text of the past finally disappeared under the interpretation? Has <i>noble posterity</i> misunderstood the whole past and in that way alone made it <i>tolerable</i> to look at?<br /><br /><b>The Free Thinker:</b><br /><br />Nietzsche is basically detailing a plan for the new philosophers—they will be men of solitude, men who can attain the <i>esoteric</i>; look down from on high where <i>good and evil</i>, <i>virtue and vice</i> cannot be so easily compartmentalized through <i>yes</i> and <i>no</i>. Above all, their search cannot be thought of as being undertaken for the good of society. Suffering cannot be eliminated, happiness cannot be guaranteed, and the new philosophers will have to face many painful <i>untruths</i> and be very courageous. They cannot limit themselves by such sentiments as <i>generosity</i> or <i>pity</i>, or like things that deplete their spirit.<br /><br /><b>On the Religious:</b><br /><br />Early Christianity comprised more <i>suffering</i>, more of a continual suicide of reason and freedom and pride, and an enslavement at the same time. The <i>saint</i> has always fascinated, because of our historic swings between <i>voluptuousness</i> and <i>asceticism</i>, and the sense of a great <i>will</i> taking control; the sense that this <i>will</i> must have had or served a <i>Powerful Purpose</i> in doing so.<br /><br /><i>The Old Testament:</i> Passionate and glorious language, violent—<i>big-souled</i>. <i>The New Testament:</i> Loving and tender, mustily abnegative—<i>small-souled</i>. The Greek religion was noble because it had a sense of great <i>gratitude</i> for life; felt that life is a <i>boon</i>.<br /><br />Modern philosophy, as epistemological skepticism, is anti-Christian but <i>not</i> anti-religious, either overtly or covertly. From a surety in the soul (the <i>"I"</i>) modern philosophy has come to wonder if the <i>"I"</i> is only apparent, that is, that the <i>think</i> may create the <i>"I"</i>.<br /><br />The three greatest of the religious rungs of cruelty are:<br /><br /><b>One:</b> Sacrifice of something dear (eg, one's firstborn) to god—this is the <i>Pre-Moral Stage.</i><br /><br /><b>Two:</b> Sacrifice of one's nature to God—this is the <i>Moral Stage.</i><br /><br /><b>Three:</b> Sacrifice of God to the Nothing—this is our <i>Modern Times</i><br /><blockquote>
  <i>It is the profound, suspicious fear of an invincible pessimism that forces whole millennia to bury their teeth in, and cling to, a religious interpretation of existence: the fear of that instinct which senses that one might get a hold of the truth too soon, before man has become strong enough, hard enough, artist enough.</i>
</blockquote><b>Aphorisms Which Gave Me An Hermeneutic Steamer:</b><br /><blockquote>
  <i>Whoever despises himself, respects himself as one who despises.<br /><br />A soul that knows it is loved but does not love itself betrays its sediment: what is at the bottom comes up.<br /><br />Heavy, heavy-spirited people become lighter precisely through what makes others heavier, through hatred and love, and for a time they surface.<br /><br />The great epochs of our life come when we gain the courage to rechristen our evil as what is best in us.<br /><br />The devil has the broadest perspectives for God; therefore he keeps so far away from God—the devil being the most ancient friend of wisdom.<br /><br />Whoever fights monsters should see to it that in the process he does not become a monster. And when you look long enough into an abyss, the abyss also looks into you.<br /><br />Whatever is done from love always occurs beyond good and evil.<br /><br />The thought of suicide is a powerful comfort: it helps one through many a dreadful night.<br /><br />The high spirits of kindness may look like malice.</i>
</blockquote><b>Natural History of Morals:</b><br /><blockquote>
  <i>Every morality is, as opposed to laisser-aller, a bit of tyranny against "nature", also against "reason".</i>
</blockquote>Nietzsche declares that morality is the setting of obedience over a lengthy period and in a single direction—philosophers and moralists <i>knew</i> the answers that they wanted before they asked questions. Morality crushed and tyrannized much of <i>nature</i> within man and made him stupid in so doing; yet it was perhaps needed for <i>spiritual discipline</i> to harden and shape European Man.<br /><br />Humans are accustomed to lying. Our senses dislike the new and prefer the familiar; our instincts, by nature, lead us to prefer the <i>truth of instincts</i>—<i>Man is much more of an artist than he knows</i>.<br /><br />Nietzsche further hammers upon the authoritarian strain in man:<br /><blockquote>
  <i>Nothing has been exercised and cultivated better and longer among men so far than obediance.</i>
</blockquote>Men have thus ingrained inside them an instinct for <i>Thou Shalt!</i><br /><blockquote>
  <i>It seizes upon things as a rude appetite, rather indiscriminately, and accepts whatever is shouted into its ears by someone who issues commands—parents, teachers, laws, class, prejudices, public opinions.<br /><br />Love thy neighbour stems from <b>fear</b> thy neighbour.<br /><br />Everything that elevates an individual above the herd and intimidates the neighbour is henceforth called <b>evil</b>.<br /><br />The imperative of herd timidity—we want that some day there should be <b>nothing anymore to be afraid of</b>. Throughout Europe, the will and way to this day is called progress.</i>
</blockquote>Nietzsche mocks the <i>Objective Man</i>—the man who accepts everything, who has no ideas of his own but is a mere vessel for the ideas and beliefs of others. He is a <i>mirror</i>, reduced to acceding or refusing, to saying <i>Yes</i> and <i>No</i>. Nietzsche further mocks the <i>Skeptic</i>—the man who doubts everything and will never say <i>Yes</i> or <i>No</i>, but ever hesitates and has thus lost his will. Doubts exists more, and skepticism flourishes, the more civilized a country has become. However, Nietzsche admires the <i>stronger and more dangerous skepticism</i> introduced to Germany and Europe by Frederick the Great—this is a virile and manly skepticism that leads to great expeditions, efforts, and exertions of the will.<br /><blockquote>
  <i>Moral judgements and condemnations constitute the favorite revenge of the spiritually limited against those less limited—also a sort of compensation for having been ill-favored by nature—finally, an opportunity for acquiring spirit and becoming refined: malice spiritualized.</i>
</blockquote><b>Our Virtues:</b><br />Modern man does not understand the devotion to things that attract the higher and choosier tastes he calls the <i>disinterested</i> action, thinking it selfless; but even the <i>highest</i> disinterested action in truth is exceedingly interested and interesting. <i>Done for love</i>—but everything done for love is egotistic. <i>Done for sacrifice</i>—but a sacrificer knows that he wanted and got something in return, perhaps a part of himself <i>for</i> a part of himself.<br /><br />Nietzsche avers that the religion of modern man is <i>Pity</i>—though through all the noise of its preachers can be discerned a sound of <i>self-contempt</i>. The man of modern ideas is immeasurably dissatisfied with himself; he suffers, and his vanity wants him—<i>needs</i> him—to suffer along with others, to feel pity.<br /><br />Sections <b>223</b> and <b>224</b> are flat-out brilliantly prophetic about the potential burgeoning of <i>multiculturalism</i>. The modern taste is for the <i>infinite</i>: we dress in the costumes of all cultures and drink the waters of all times—we cannot stand and measure things, but long for, crave the <i>eternal</i>, even as our instincts, in the play of chaos, run towards the past. Limitations or highest valuations seem an infernal stasis, while the boundless horizon, abandonment, the primal and unmeasured appears rife with the promise and thrill of life.<br /><br />The <i>Last Men</i> wish to abolish suffering: the <i>Higher Men</i> hold that to be an end that makes man contemptible and ridiculous. The discipline of suffering is what allows Man to transcend his limits. Man is both <i>Created</i> and <i>Creator</i>, and so necessarily <i>should</i> and <i>must</i> suffer; be broken, burned, and reformed, that he might <i>create</i> to overcome himself.<br /><br />Nietzsche explains how cruelty has always been needed for man to break through his comfortable thoughts and notions: the spirit is all too happy to assume masks to maintain illusions—sometimes the will revels in ignorance. The cruelty of intellectual conscience must be able to break or shatter these other wills in order for man to grow.<br /><br />The demoralization of Europe is making races and peoples more alike—is the flattening and the mediocritization of man. This new herd society of good workers will thus be comprised of people as in need of command as of sustenance—the masses will be produced for <i>slavery</i> in the subtlest sense. But this will allow select individuals to become stronger and richer than ever before imaginable—an involuntary arrangement for the cultivation of <i>tyrants</i>, taking that word even in the spiritual sense.<br /><br /><b>Peoples and Fatherlands:</b><br /><br />Nietzsche says Wagner wrote perfect <i>German</i> music for his day—it looked fondly at tomorrow whilst yearning for the past; the Germans were people of yesterday and tomorrow—they had no <i>today</i>. Mozart and Beethoven wrote music for <i>Europe</i>, changed Europe. With the birth of the Romantics, German music became dangerous, a music not of Europe but rather of <i>Fatherlands</i>.<br /><br />Nietzsche further finds the English to be the un-philosophical people, the vulgar nation, creator and implementer of <i>Modern Ideas</i>. They are non-musical—and hence succumbed to <i>The Spirit of Gravity</i> from <i>Zarathustra</i>. The French are the creators of <i>Noble</i> ideas in Europe, but their soul is being corrupted by the <i>small virtues</i> of the English.<br /><br /><b>What is Noble:</b><br /><br />Nietzsche holds that all enhancements of man have arisen within an aristocratic society: a noble caste of barbarians, strong in will to power and uncorrupted by <i>anarchy among the instincts</i>, takes advantage of the inherent exploitation that is a primordial fact of all history.<br /><br /><i>Master Morality</i> and <i>Slave Morality</i> often exist, side-by-side or intermingled, in modern man. <i>Master Morality</i> is value-creating, one wherein good and bad are paired as <i>noble vs contemptible</i>. It creates value from oneself, not from outside judgements. It provides aid to the weak from an excess of power, the source of gifts, never from pity. Pity is contemptible. A hardness of heart is needed. <i>Slave Morality</i> bears the marked distinction of being a morality of pity, acting for others <i>disinterestedly</i>, ie from selflessness, compassionate feelings and a warm heart.<br /><br /><i>Master Morality</i> possesses a reverence for age and tradition; personal honour in doing one's duties, of which said duty is only to one's peers, for friendships and enmities. The lower castes are treated beyond Good and Evil—but duty demands that pity be given its appropriate place downwards. <i>Slave Morality</i> looks to progress and the future, and disrespects tradition. All the world are brothers and equals—pity is extended and extendable to <i>everyone</i>. It is inherently suspicious towards the values of a <i>Master Morality</i>, and is invariably a morality of <i>utility</i>: industry, happiness, friendship, compassion. It is designed specifically to ease suffering.<br /><br />In <i>Master Morality</i>, the Good inspire, and wish to inspire, fear. The Bad is the contemptible. In <i>Slave Morality</i>, The Evil are those who inspire fear. However, the Good in a <i>Slave Morality</i> has also to inspire some degree of contempt, because the Good is not dangerous. In all <i>Slave Morality</i> cultures the language tends to bring closer together the words <i>Good</i> and <i>Stupid</i>.<br /><br />The noble man arises from an area beset by nature and/or neighbours. Only a tradition of hard, firm, unchanging laws and severity allows the state's continued existence; only when one no longer exists within such life and death environs does that hardness thaw and people begin to prize the individual. Corruption sets in as all individuals inevitably learn to preach mediocrity.<br /><br />The noble soul <i>is</i> egoism—it has no vanity and only treats <i>thoughtfully</i> with its peers. The noble soul is used to looking straight ahead, slowly, or down: rarely does it stray upwards because it already <i>knows itself to be at a height</i>. It is not vain because it doesn't require the good opinion of others to provide it with a self-confidence or self-esteem it does not have already have. Support from others is <i>alien</i> to the noble soul.<br /><br />Nietzsche is saying herein that the noble soul has <i>reverence</i> for itself. Many aspire to be noble—and none can say <i>who</i> will be a genius and great—but this self-honour must be <i>innate</i>. Everywhere that men gather, there also will gather the urge to <i>commonality</i>. The noble soul will be drawn to what are virtues for it: solitude, courage, insight, sympathy. <i>Raphael, born without hands, would still be a great artist!</i><br /><br />The perception through illusion and mask that true life is a great and endless <i>suffering</i> causes <i>nausea</i>—the non-noble soul will choke on this nausea and learn to loathe himself and life; the noble soul will spit out the nausea and continue revering life.<br /><br />Man invented the <i>good conscience</i> in order to be able to enjoy his soul as <i>simple</i>. In this way the entirety of his morality is an enduringly opaque veil allowing him enjoyment of the sight of the soul.<br /><blockquote>
  <i>[Dionysus] once said: "Under certain circumstances I love what is human"—and with this he alluded to Ariadne who was present—"man is to my mind an agreeable, courageous, inventive animal that has no equal on earth; it finds its way in any labyrinth. I am well disposed towards him: I often reflect how I might yet advance him and make him stronger, more evil, and more profound than he is."<br /><br />"Stronger, more evil, and more profound?" I asked startled. "Yes," he said once more; "stronger, more evil, and more profound; also more beautiful"—and at that the tempter god smiled with his halcyon smile as though he had just paid an enchanting compliment. Here we also see: what this divinity lacks is not only a sense of shame—and there are also other good reasons for conjecturing that in several respects all of the gods could learn from us humans. We humans are—more humane.</i>
</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="14409205158512784971" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer2023294449" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer18064232540104924794">Beyond Good and Evil has made for an informative and thought-provoking experience. I did not appreciate this as much as The Antichrist, but still, this book holds some rather valuable points, and I'm glad to have finally read it. <br/><br/>To appreciate Nietzsche, it helps to not be easily offended. I am aware of people that are put off by something that he may have stated in his writings, and therefore, they just make the rather odd presumption, that Nietzsche was in fact, a moron. As a feminist, it is e</span>
  <span id="freeText18064232540104924794" style="display:none">Beyond Good and Evil has made for an informative and thought-provoking experience. I did not appreciate this as much as The Antichrist, but still, this book holds some rather valuable points, and I'm glad to have finally read it. <br /><br />To appreciate Nietzsche, it helps to not be easily offended. I am aware of people that are put off by something that he may have stated in his writings, and therefore, they just make the rather odd presumption, that Nietzsche was in fact, a moron. As a feminist, it is evident that Nietzsche was a misogynist, and there are parts in this book where he makes this known, but there are also parts, where he discusses equality of the sexes. I notice that Nietzsche admits that his misogyny is in fact a terrible weakness of his.<br /><br />The style of this book is written in typical Nietzsche, so if you are expecting something that flows smoothly, you won't find that here. This is written in note form, although, the subject matter is in some sort of order that one can gain something from.<br /><br />"Whoever fights monsters should see to it that in the process he does not become a monster. And when you look long into an abyss, the abyss also looks into you." <br /><br />I enjoy how Nietzsche can write a line of thoughts, and then that can prompt a series of interesting questions and answers from his readers. I'll certainly be reading more from him in the future.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18064232540104924794" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_2560129750" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2560129750" />
    <a title="Lia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/44005555-lia"><img alt="Lia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1578446760p2/44005555.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2560129750?book_show_action=true">Dec 16, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lia" href="/user/show/44005555-lia">Lia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780679724650" href="/book/show/529326.Beyond_Good_and_Evil">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44005555-lia?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44005555-lia?shelf=nietzsche">nietzsche</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2560129750" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5494219875720489965">The hardest part of this whole process is to declare this book as "read". I'm not done with it. I've reread chapters, flipped back and forth to weave the necessary web to link up the scattered pieces, the clues. I've reread and re-interpreted aphorisms over and over... how can I say I'm "done" when I'm only becoming acquainted? <br/><br/>I wish I have something conclusive and clever to say about this book, but the only conclusive thing I can come up with is that this book treats you like a beast of burden</span>
  <span id="freeText5494219875720489965" style="display:none">The hardest part of this whole process is to declare this book as "read". I'm not done with it. I've reread chapters, flipped back and forth to weave the necessary web to link up the scattered pieces, the clues. I've reread and re-interpreted aphorisms over and over... how can I say I'm "done" when I'm only becoming acquainted? <br /><br />I wish I have something conclusive and clever to say about this book, but the only conclusive thing I can come up with is that this book treats you like a beast of burden, an enduring ass. It makes you work, and patiently trace back, rework. I'm not the same person now that I've "traversed" the book, the process of struggling with this book changed me, the book itself changed for me, passages no longer mean the same thing to me, but there're still many more puzzles to solve, more works to do.<br /><br />It may not be a good idea to keep working through the labyrinth though. Labyrinths are built to detain beasts; tracing the clues to its ultimate conclusion, to unconceal, led Oedipus to his final fate. <br /><br />The book ends with a poem, and the poem ends with laughter. That's what Oedipus should have done, when facing the Sphinx. I'm going to laugh ... for now. I may come back later... if it turns out I am a tempter, an attempter.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5494219875720489965" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_31313975" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/31313975" />
    <a title="Brad" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1022982-brad"><img alt="Brad" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442484575p2/1022982.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/31313975?book_show_action=true">Aug 27, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brad" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brad" href="/user/show/1022982-brad">Brad</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780679724650" href="/book/show/529326.Beyond_Good_and_Evil">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer31313975" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer482665103376479981">Although not what I expected, Nietzsche's <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12321.Beyond_Good_and_Evil' title='Beyond Good and Evil by Friedrich Nietzsche' rel='nofollow noopener'>Beyond Good and Evil</a> was a more than satisfying meditation on morality. It wasn't what I expected because most of Nietzsche's words were spent prophesying about and discussing the "herd" mentality of democracy's slave culture, which prepares us for his final, magnificent essay, "What is Noble," but the overthrowing of my expectations was never a problem.<br/><br/>Too many pass over Nietzsche because they are pre-offended, missing what is powerful and vital in his </span>
  <span id="freeText482665103376479981" style="display:none">Although not what I expected, Nietzsche's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12321.Beyond_Good_and_Evil" title="Beyond Good and Evil by Friedrich Nietzsche" rel="nofollow noopener">Beyond Good and Evil</a> was a more than satisfying meditation on morality. It wasn't what I expected because most of Nietzsche's words were spent prophesying about and discussing the "herd" mentality of democracy's slave culture, which prepares us for his final, magnificent essay, "What is Noble," but the overthrowing of my expectations was never a problem.<br /><br />Too many pass over Nietzsche because they are pre-offended, missing what is powerful and vital in his writings without ever giving it a try. Some of the offense is a product of his time, some is a product of his cultural context, some is the misrepresentations about his influence on Nazi thought, and some is simply the indoctrinated discomfort bred into those who try and read him today. Being offended by Nietzsche is a shame. There is much of importance in his work, particularly for those who feel they are -- like Zarathustra -- screaming into a vast vacuum.<br /><br />For anyone willing to dig deep, Nietzsche will speak to you.</span>
  <a data-text-id="482665103376479981" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

              <span id="reviewTextContainer497417959" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6589914001267826558">The passage which really summed up this book for me was "Every deep thinker is more afraid of being understood than of being misunderstood." Yep, right there. It's what annoys me about a lot of philosophy - I just want people to be able to write clearly and honestly about what they actually mean. Nietzsche's language is so dense and impenetrable (and clearly deliberately so) that it is frustrating to read. There's definitely a whiff of the emperor's new clothes about this book.<br/><br/>And don't get me s</span>
  <span id="freeText6589914001267826558" style="display:none">The passage which really summed up this book for me was "Every deep thinker is more afraid of being understood than of being misunderstood." Yep, right there. It's what annoys me about a lot of philosophy - I just want people to be able to write clearly and honestly about what they actually mean. Nietzsche's language is so dense and impenetrable (and clearly deliberately so) that it is frustrating to read. There's definitely a whiff of the emperor's new clothes about this book.<br /><br />And don't get me started on his views about women: "nothing is more foreign, more repugnant, or more hostile to woman than truth - her great art is falsehood, her chief concern is appearance and beauty." Oh dear, too late, I can't stop now: "When a woman has scholarly inclinations there is generally something wrong with her sexual nature. Barrenness itself conduces to a certain virility of taste...".<br /><br />"Comparing man and woman generally, one may say that woman would not have the genius for adornment, if she had not the instinct for the <em>secondary</em> role."<br /><br />I thought Erasmus's views were bad, but he lived four hundred years before Nietzsche. I had hoped that by the late nineteenth century 'deep thinkers' might have become more enlightened. Apparently not.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6589914001267826558" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_2442316805" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2442316805" />
    <a title="David Huff" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22679327-david-huff"><img alt="David Huff" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1567382988p2/22679327.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2442316805?book_show_action=true">Jul 05, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Huff" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Huff" href="/user/show/22679327-david-huff">David Huff</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2442316805" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16724792233567073510">“Dur Wille zur Macht” (the Will to Power): what Nietzsche saw as the prime motivator in the lives of mankind: ambition, achievement, the struggle to reach as high as possible with one’s life. Often contrasted with Viktor Frankl’s view (Meaning) and Freud’s view (Pleasure).<br/><br/>This was one of my main takeaways from “Beyond Good and Evil” (BGE), my first foray into Nietzsche’s writings. BGE is a series of 9 essays and a concluding poem (“Aftersong”). Each essay is subdivided into smaller, consecutivel</span>
  <span id="freeText16724792233567073510" style="display:none">“Dur Wille zur Macht” (the Will to Power): what Nietzsche saw as the prime motivator in the lives of mankind: ambition, achievement, the struggle to reach as high as possible with one’s life. Often contrasted with Viktor Frankl’s view (Meaning) and Freud’s view (Pleasure).<br /><br />This was one of my main takeaways from “Beyond Good and Evil” (BGE), my first foray into Nietzsche’s writings. BGE is a series of 9 essays and a concluding poem (“Aftersong”). Each essay is subdivided into smaller, consecutively numbered sections, in lengths ranging from one sentence to 3 or 4 pages. Nietzsche was, in fact, fond of short aphorisms, and essay #4 (“Epigrams and Interludes) is composed solely of these one or two sentence sayings. While few of them will likely ever make it to a motivational poster, they’re thought provoking just the same.<br /><br />Like many, I had heard or read things about Nietzsche over the years, but until now had never actually read him. I knew, going in, that his worldview was markedly different than my own, but wanted to experience his thinking firsthand. My impressions: – he was a man of powerful intellect, bold and outspoken, holding back little in his sometimes withering critique of other philosophers. He was no friend of religion, though surprisingly in BGE he had good words for the Jews and ironically criticized his own Germany for its growing antisemitism.<br /><br />Nietzsche’s writing can be pointed and sometimes dense, but seldom dull and often quite stylish. He actually is known for being a particularly “readable” philosopher. Sometimes he can sound rebellious, or petulant, even arrogant, all of which spring from the deep passion of his beliefs. He is known for several key concepts, including the eternal recurrence, master-slave morality, the will to power (mentioned above), and others, some of which find expression in BGE.<br /><br />This was a mind-expanding and interesting read, much of which I’m sure I didn’t fully grasp, but worthwhile nonetheless. Translator Walter Kaufmann describes this book well: “It is possible to say briefly what makes the book great: the prophetic independence of its spirit; the hundreds of doors it opens for the mind, revealing new vistas, problems, and relationships; and what it contributes to our understanding of much of recent thought and literature and history”.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16724792233567073510" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer8584124210050462106">4.0 stars.       </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4321301-nemo?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer698493259" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16883637118044497627"><blockquote>With a philosopher nothing at all is impersonal.</blockquote><br/>As an armchair Platonist, I had a personal aversion to Nietzsche, whose whole purpose in life seemed to be to overthrow Platonism. After reading "Beyond Good and Evil", however, my attitude changed from aversion to pity, that is, pity in the Nietzschean sense.<br/><br/>To illustrate my view of Nietzsche and his relation to Plato, let me introduce a Chinese fictional/mythical character, <a href='http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun_Wukong' rel='nofollow noopener'>Sun Wukong (孙悟空)</a>, also known as the Monkey King. The Monkey King challeng</span>
  <span id="freeText16883637118044497627" style="display:none"><blockquote>With a philosopher nothing at all is impersonal.</blockquote><br />As an armchair Platonist, I had a personal aversion to Nietzsche, whose whole purpose in life seemed to be to overthrow Platonism. After reading "Beyond Good and Evil", however, my attitude changed from aversion to pity, that is, pity in the Nietzschean sense.<br /><br />To illustrate my view of Nietzsche and his relation to Plato, let me introduce a Chinese fictional/mythical character, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun_Wukong" rel="nofollow noopener">Sun Wukong (孙悟空)</a>, also known as the Monkey King. The Monkey King challenged the authority of the gods, stormed their dwelling, The Heavenly Palace, and proclaimed himself an equal of the gods. They appealed to the Buddha for help, after repeatedly failing to subdue him. The Buddha made a wager with the Monkey King, who could travel 108,000 li with one somersault, that the latter could not jump out of the former's palm. In order to prove his power, the Monkey King traveled as far as he could, and reached what he thought were the Five Pillars of Heaven. When he returned to confront the Buddha, however, he learned to his chagrin that those pillars were actually the Buddha's fingers. He lost and was imprisoned by the Buddha under a mountain for 500 years.<br /><br />An attentive reader would have no difficulty guessing at my meaning: Nietzsche was the Monkey King, Plato the Buddha.<br /><br /><b>Five-Point Critique</b><br /><br />Firstly, Plato derived the notion of an eternal cyclic nature of the universe long before Nietzsche stumbled upon it and gave it a different name, "eternal recurrence". Apparently, like the Monkey King, Nietzsche was not immune to self-deception and delusions of grandeur, when he claimed that his philosophy was new and free of metaphysical presumptions.<br /><br />Secondly, there is nothing new to the idea of "order of rank" either. Plato made a division of classes in his <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/30289.The_Republic" title="The Republic by Plato" rel="nofollow noopener">Republic</a>. Nietzsche seems to share Plato's contempt for democracy, which is based on the assumption of equality among man. Both would assert that some men are fit to rule and others to be ruled.<br /><br />Thirdly, Christianity has long inculcated the notion that suffering is necessary for the character development of human beings. Nietzsche borrowed the idea again, without acknowledging the source.<br /><br />Fourthly, Nietzsche's philosophy is not grounded in biological facts, but rather another subjective interpretation with assumptions and leaps. To use his own simile, the text may have disappeared under the interpretation, but it is still there, and each interpretation shall be evaluated according to its relation to the original. The philosopher can no more place himself above the standard of good and evil, than a translator can place himself above the original.<br /><br />Fifthly, the ancient Greek philosophers believed that the ultimate purpose of philosophy is the attainment of the Good and the True. Nietzsche rejected the notion as utilitarian and ignoble. What noble value did he create by will to power that would set him above those philosophers he satirized? None.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16883637118044497627" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_103139951" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/103139951" />
    <a title="Ryan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/831864-ryan"><img alt="Ryan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1590352098p2/831864.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/103139951?book_show_action=true">May 17, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ryan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ryan" href="/user/show/831864-ryan">Ryan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780521779135" href="/book/show/451561.Beyond_Good_and_Evil">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer103139951" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5001119199657238071">As always, Nietzsche presents a difficult, possibly contradictory array of views on the subjects of society, morality and history. I am certain that he wouldn't take offense to our picking-and-choosing among his philosophy- he wouldn't want to be taken dogmatically. To suggest that we find splendid truth in his writing alongside heinous invective would probably please him. He certainly wouldn't claim to have a monopoly on truth and wants us to come to our own conclusions.<br/><br/>In keeping with his othe</span>
  <span id="freeText5001119199657238071" style="display:none">As always, Nietzsche presents a difficult, possibly contradictory array of views on the subjects of society, morality and history. I am certain that he wouldn't take offense to our picking-and-choosing among his philosophy- he wouldn't want to be taken dogmatically. To suggest that we find splendid truth in his writing alongside heinous invective would probably please him. He certainly wouldn't claim to have a monopoly on truth and wants us to come to our own conclusions.<br /><br />In keeping with his other works that I've read, I am most moved by his vehement affirmation of life. He sees the dominant culture of his time as shying away from and condemning life and all its difficulties. In contrast, he strives to not only accept life in all its pain and struggle, but to forever cry for more. His idea of "eternal return" emphasizes the point- that we should live as though this life will be repeated ad infinitum. Nietzsche displays a veritable thirst for life, and its struggles only invigorate him.<br /><br />Of course, we must address the troubling case of the misogyny displayed in the latter quarter of this book. It should be expected that when one so readily denounces conventional morality and democracy, and accepts that some will be born superior and some inferior, and that racial and national traits are real and inherited, well, it should come as no surprise that he embraces misogyny. Rather than discard all his works in disgust, I think we can learn something from this case. No doubt there is truth in much of what he writes, so we must ask ourselves where he went wrong in order to take such a heinous turn. Where in his philosophy lies the root of these opinions?<br /><br />Many have seen the basis of these problematic pronouncements in Nietzsche's personal &amp; psychological history. I suspect that were he alive today, he would have to grapple with the body of scientific evidence that suggests his broad opinions are based not in fact but in socially constructed roles. Nietzsche made himself vulnerable to believing in the reality of "national character" and "gender identity" and "racial memory" and all of these distractions, probably believing all along that he was speaking truth to power (an uncommon and unpopular "truth", his favorite sort.) <br /><br />In the end, we do not have to agree or disagree wholesale with Niezsche's philosophy. As with all things, there are bits of value and bits to discard. In examining what we discard and why, we can learn from Nietzsche's mistakes.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5001119199657238071" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_183113944" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/183113944" />
    <a title="John Martindale" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4771466-john-martindale"><img alt="John Martindale" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1521951492p2/4771466.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/183113944?book_show_action=true">Jul 08, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="John Martindale" class="user" itemprop="url" name="John Martindale" href="/user/show/4771466-john-martindale">John Martindale</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140445138" href="/book/show/448894.Beyond_Good_and_Evil">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4771466-john-martindale?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4771466-john-martindale?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4771466-john-martindale?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer183113944" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17787493569422651468">Nietzsche is for the atheist what Charles Spurgeon was for Christian preachers. He has a creative way of saying things and this book is filled with one liners. He makes me think of a preacher, in that he says extreme things with absolute confidence, but does not back anything up or go into much depth. This book seemed to me not so much about going beyond good and evil, but rather a justification of evil. Alexander, Napoleon, Hitler, Stalin and Mao in their rejecting the "slave" morality and embr</span>
  <span id="freeText17787493569422651468" style="display:none">Nietzsche is for the atheist what Charles Spurgeon was for Christian preachers. He has a creative way of saying things and this book is filled with one liners. He makes me think of a preacher, in that he says extreme things with absolute confidence, but does not back anything up or go into much depth. This book seemed to me not so much about going beyond good and evil, but rather a justification of evil. Alexander, Napoleon, Hitler, Stalin and Mao in their rejecting the "slave" morality and embracing the pursuit of power, could have felt virtuous and good about themselves. If power corrupts and absolutely power corrupts absolutely, I got the impression that in the mind of Nietzsche, this absolute corruption is the highest end for man, for power justifies everything! If I am not completely misinterpreting what he says (I may be) then what a disgusting philosophy and how scary it is, that it resonates with people! But I suppose if "God is dead" and therefore mankind and the universe is the mere product of blind unguided processes, then maybe what Nietzsche presents here logically follows. We got to see Nietzsche's philosophy lived out to its fullest in WWII. Hitler loved Nietzsche writings, in fact most of Germany embraced his philosophy as a way of life (watch the documentary "Neitzsche and the Nazis" by Stephen Hicks). The war loving Nietzsche, had he lived to see it, would have seen all these enlightened, Nazi free-spirits killing the weak and surely would have felt proud. And wow, how could woman like this guy? His chapter on woman was something else. If you are a woman, according to this beloved philosopher, you can't think and never should even be allowed to try, your only purpose as the weaker vessel is to have babies, that's it! </span>
  <a data-text-id="17787493569422651468" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3410912622" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3410912622" />
    <a title="Luís" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/117399210-lu-s"><img alt="Luís" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598708286p2/117399210.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3410912622?book_show_action=true">Jun 25, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Luís" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Luís" href="/user/show/117399210-lu-s">Luís</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9726652499" href="/book/show/17264038-para-al-m-de-bem-e-mal">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/117399210-lu-s?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/117399210-lu-s?shelf=german-literature">german-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/117399210-lu-s?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/117399210-lu-s?shelf=religion">religion</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/117399210-lu-s?shelf=guimaraes">guimaraes</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/117399210-lu-s?shelf=psychology">psychology</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3410912622" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16355055113974932234">In "Beyond Good and Evil", Nietzsche does not go with a grain of salt. His criticism of the democratic principle of equality leads him to take very radical positions which could offend individual humanist minds. This criticism would be (this is my interpretation) a logical continuation of the Christian principles of pity and charity. Indeed, to preserve themselves from the disgust of life and not to sink into an incurable pessimism, men hide from the truth through these principles defined as use</span>
  <span id="freeText16355055113974932234" style="display:none">In "Beyond Good and Evil", Nietzsche does not go with a grain of salt. His criticism of the democratic principle of equality leads him to take very radical positions which could offend individual humanist minds. This criticism would be (this is my interpretation) a logical continuation of the Christian principles of pity and charity. Indeed, to preserve themselves from the disgust of life and not to sink into an incurable pessimism, men hide from the truth through these principles defined as useful. We thus understand why men are false and inconstant. Equality is only an illusion, the hide-and-seek of a world that advances by what Nietzsche calls the will of power. There are beings, whom he calls "aristocratic", seeking to flourish through oppression on other beings, by far the most numerous, desiring them to submit to great leaders, through all-powerful masters. Isn't a nice picture?<br />But its most stimulating aphorisms are those dealing with a kind of relativism: any theory being only interpretation, even scientific laws, then there is no lasting and immutable truth. And as, moreover, our senses are deceptive, they cannot give us the keys to a sovereign reality. And he also does not hesitate to warn us about the truths he teaches us: these are his current truths, they will no longer once written down on paper.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16355055113974932234" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1450544036" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1450544036" />
    <a title="Crito" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/35959535-crito"><img alt="Crito" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1413342130p2/35959535.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1450544036?book_show_action=true">Oct 26, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Crito" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Crito" href="/user/show/35959535-crito">Crito</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780679724650" href="/book/show/529326.Beyond_Good_and_Evil">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35959535-crito?shelf=2016-goals">2016-goals</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35959535-crito?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1450544036" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3120491061595996240">I would like to embrace the older writeup below as an example of what a weak reading of Nietzsche looks like. One of Nietzsche's major lines of arguments is that philosophy is a set of simplifications in part motivated by the will to ignorance; that is one makes oneself skeptical only to pursue an ideal of a philosophical system which will satisfy all questions in which the inquiry ceases. Nietzsche however firmly believes that is a simplification and indeed the onward progress is to critique an</span>
  <span id="freeText3120491061595996240" style="display:none">I would like to embrace the older writeup below as an example of what a weak reading of Nietzsche looks like. One of Nietzsche's major lines of arguments is that philosophy is a set of simplifications in part motivated by the will to ignorance; that is one makes oneself skeptical only to pursue an ideal of a philosophical system which will satisfy all questions in which the inquiry ceases. Nietzsche however firmly believes that is a simplification and indeed the onward progress is to critique and disrupt the older simplifications for a set of new ones, the problem being that philosophy is so wonderful at tearing apart values but has difficulty in creating new values. Unlike some characterizations of him he is a cheerleader of the enlightenment, tear everything apart he says, but then his critique is that the enlightenment is not prepared for its own god, truth, to be put under philosophical scrutiny and skepticism. So from this standpoint, while there are familiar arguments you might find in Rousseau, this is entirely novel and you can see why not only does he not systematize but it makes absolutely no sense for him to. This isn't Humean skepticism, this isn't a collection of quips; this is an attempt to supplant former philosophies but not in the manner in which they have so far presented themselves. Nietzsche doesn't exempt himself from the simplification charge. But he distinguishes from Platonic style philosophy which seeks to defer outside the world, British materialist philosophy which simply takes the world at face value, and his kind of philosophy, one which stays rooted in the world but explains our assumptions akin to Kepler explaining why it appears as if the sun rotates around the earth while demonstrating the opposite. And furthermore he sees pitfalls but major promise in the German philosophy of development; at worst it arrests values from ever developing and taking shape, but at its best (and in moments of cultural disintegration and change) the great value creators can emerge, the Alcibiadeses, the Caesars, the Da Vincis. He is timely in that sense, and in how German thought has fertilized French (himself, Heidegger, Frankfurts) and unexpectedly Anglo (Frege, Wittgenstein) thought. My critique of his view of women still stands but with the qualification that many times he uses terms like woman or feminine or feminism not to talk about anything relating to women, but rather to make points about truth with some admittedly boomer metaphors. Other times he means women as women. Some take issue with his style, but in here he outright tells you Germans have the problem of being really fucking boring writers, that the best stylist prior has been the sober faced Luther of all people. You start to appreciate how good a writer Nietzsche is after a while, but it is something to get used to if it doesn't grab you right away. And his tendency to circle around and return to the subjects of his critique does make this harder to read than straightforward treatises and it takes a few books to really get his angle. The ones I'd recommend are the essay On Truth and Lies, The Gay Science, and the Genealogy of Morals, and then you might be more receptive to this one. It's an admittable point of contention that you need to spread yourself out with him rather than relying on one text. But otherwise he hits on some salient and original things, especially in this one. It takes living with the prejudices of philosophy for a while to really appreciate what he's trying to say about them here, so I might recommend this for revisiting if it happened to bounce off you on your first read too. Kaufmann is a good place to go because of his own fight against misinterpretation. This work does deserve to be taken seriously, though "joyfully" in his sense.<br /><br />_______________________________<br />Admittedly, Nietzsche grabs you immediately. His writing has a sensational style that is both lucid and over the top, his words spark on almost every page. For you folks who like your quotables they're everywhere here. But as you get deeper in, there's something startling in that it doesn't seem like he's saying much at all. That is, if you've followed the history of philosophy up to this point, there's nothing incredibly original here, it's just the way he says it. One of the large takeaways is the idea that philosophy (and society by extension) has all sorts of conceptions that it doesn't realize it takes for granted. While this is completely agreeable and something any thinker should readily contemplate, how is this new? Well what's new about it is simply timeliness. Nietzsche was placed right where the industrial revolution was dramatically changing the makeup of the world and its culture, and Nietzsche was then saying the right things at the right time, and in such a spectacular fashion as to make people notice.<br />This however doesn't put Nietzsche as a thinker, but rather as a sayer. He's just saying things. He has aspirations of a philosopher but he doesn't build an actual system of philosophy as any other philosopher would. He borrows Hume's sledgehammer and cherrypicks bits of Hegel and Schopenhauer to sprinkle on the ensuing rubble. There's no subtlety of thought, even in the continuity of his worldview. He does much to establish that the morals, values, and traditions of old are all limits beset on an individual to ensnare them into never fulfilling their potential, and then proceeds to rant about how women were made for subservience due to innate "superficiality"; he doesn't consider that was possibly the result of the system of arbitrary values he so loathes which prevent women from getting an education, and that they too could achieve the ascendancy he advocates for by going through his ideas of breaking societal tethers. Note I'm not saying he's bad cause he's sexist and so on, it's that he's seemingly incompetent when it comes to applying his worldview to his own worldview. And that goes back to the fact that he doesn't explore or build systems and arguments: he just says things.<br />This is why I think Zarathustra was so successful. It wasn't trying to be an explicit philosophical text, it was something more than that. It took full advantage of his sense of grandeur and mystery, his irony and humor, his ultimate ideals. Beyond Good and Evil though is just a bunch of messy unstructured essays delivered from on top a soapbox. I'm being harsh here, but that's just because I expected more than that. It works fine out of the context of philosophy. A lot of non philosophers have writings like this and they're excellent because it's their discernment, interpretation, and ability to express themselves that matters, not the rigors that philosophy pull in. Nietzsche has all of those strengths and for that I'd give it a light recommendation with a disclaimer not to take him too seriously.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3120491061595996240" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_928271816" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/928271816" />
    <a title="Justin Evans" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2184529-justin-evans"><img alt="Justin Evans" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1319658063p2/2184529.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/928271816?book_show_action=true">May 04, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Justin Evans" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Justin Evans" href="/user/show/2184529-justin-evans">Justin Evans</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780199537075" href="/book/show/3426918-beyond-good-and-evil">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2184529-justin-evans?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer928271816" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15345238078825648955">Utterly meaningless star rating alert! BGE is really a great book, the best place to start with Nietzsche, I think, because it states his most important ideas in digestible chunks (unlike Zarathustra, which is so over-wrought and self-regarding that I have trouble even flicking through it), and has no aspirations towards unity (and so is unlike Genealogy of Morality, which achieves that unity at the price of being transparently silly). Friedrich works best in paragraphs, and that's what he gives</span>
  <span id="freeText15345238078825648955" style="display:none">Utterly meaningless star rating alert! BGE is really a great book, the best place to start with Nietzsche, I think, because it states his most important ideas in digestible chunks (unlike Zarathustra, which is so over-wrought and self-regarding that I have trouble even flicking through it), and has no aspirations towards unity (and so is unlike Genealogy of Morality, which achieves that unity at the price of being transparently silly). Friedrich works best in paragraphs, and that's what he gives us here. He's about infinity times more enjoyable to read than any other philosopher, ever; I've never felt that more than this reading of BGE. <br /><br />Now, that said, his reasoning is transparently flawed. He fails to treat themes outside of his own the way he treats his pet themes. To wit: why is he so keen to discuss the 'history' of Christianity, but so unwilling to discuss the history of misogyny? He never acknowledges the obvious absurdities of his positions--if all people create their reality by interpretation, why assume that there is any 'natural' given (which he does with, e.g., instinct)? If all people create their reality by interpretation, why should we value Nietzsche's interpretation rather than the (then) reigning liberal Christianism? <br /><br />These inconsistencies make it plain that he thinks his thought is different from previous philosophies; "every great philosophy to date has been the personal confession of its author." It's equally obvious that his work, more than any other (possible exception: Rousseau) is driven by his own idiosyncrasies.<br /><br />This urge to be a unique snowflake drives BGE. Nietzsche starts by separating himself from other 'free thinkers.' He refuses to martyr himself to the 'truth' of atheism. He's beyond both utilitarianism, and Kantianism; he has overcome all morals; he has shifted the burden of proof to those who say that doing good by others is a good thing. He's beyond the enlightenment, which assumed a *good* world, and beyond Christians, who assume a bad one; he assumes a mutable, already interpreted world. <br /><br />But he's very slippery on this--sometimes he wants to get underneath all that interpretation to the text itself; sometimes he values the interpretation rather than the text; sometimes he implies that there is no text. The real 'free spirits' will be experimenters, ultra-individualistic, and ultra-elitist. Whereas the free-thinkers (to borrow from Berlin) aim for negative freedom, Nietzsche and the future philosophers aim for positive freedom. <br /><br />Sadly for Nietzsche, who was deeply oppressed by pretty much everything, religion was keeping down the free spirits. On the other hand, it did train the great to be great and fool the weak into submission. I'm curious to know what Nietzsche would have thought of the Ayn Rands and neo-Darwinians who use materialism, atheism and economics to achieve the same ends. <br /><br />Thus far, act one. Now for act two. <br /><br />In section 5, we get a shorter, less silly version of the Genealogy of Morality, with some of the same problems of reflexivity (we should do a genealogical reading of philosophies of morality... but not Nietzsche's. All young sciences make outrageous, unprovable claims due to a lack of skepticism... except Nietzsche's). Christianity is foolish... but Nietzsche's crazed messianism is legitimate. <br /><br />True philosophers, we learn, are just like Nietzsche, whereas scholars are (presumably) the people who didn't like Birth of Tragedy. Philosophers create values, rather than justifying pre-existing values; they are opposed to their own times. The new philosophical virtues will be pluralism, spite, dissection, hierarchy, self-interest, individualism for the elite, absence of pity, laughter, good taste, dutiful immoralism, honest harshness, cruelty, misogyny, and not being English. <br /><br />As a friend of mine put it about later philosophers with similar ideas, there will always be an audience for this, because there will always be self-impressed teenagers. <br /><br />The final section is probably the best, and reveals Nietzsche at his worst: a small, lonely, intelligent man, so filled with rage at his own failures that he has to invent a mythology that will justify his own sense of superiority. He has a different morality to others, he is a master; we, the peons, have a slave morality. One can only wish that the masters would really retire to write bilious pamphlets instead of taking over the commanding heights of the world economy, where they create values that Nietzsche would have loathed, and which, perhaps, would have led him to think the better of his more adolescent fantasies of self-sufficient genius.<br /><br />But despite the inconsistencies and absurdities and misogyny, BGE is filled with legitimately fascinating and important insights. The irony of Nietzsche's career is that he's at his best when undermining others; that is, when he's being a critic, rather than a creator of new values etc... His 'creation' is nothing other than an inversion of whatever other people say (they say be good to others; he says be good to oneself) with some post-positivist blather about nature and evolution. His criticisms, though, are devastating for much of the philosophical tradition. If only he'd stuck to that, and recognized the main meta-philosophical lesson of Hegel: the owl of Minerva flies at dusk. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15345238078825648955" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_874231913" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/874231913" />
    <a title="Rhonda" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1792361-rhonda"><img alt="Rhonda" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1230767995p2/1792361.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/874231913?book_show_action=true">Mar 06, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rhonda" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rhonda" href="/user/show/1792361-rhonda">Rhonda</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781495386879" href="/book/show/20793332-beyond-good-and-evil">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1792361-rhonda?shelf=psychology">psychology</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1792361-rhonda?shelf=sociology">sociology</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1792361-rhonda?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer874231913" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2653167053919338256">I have been hesitant to write this review simply because I have had so much fun re-reading this book, one which I consider, despite its small size, one of the most influential books on philosophy of the late 19th century. It is rare that one gets the chance to laugh at a philosopher's depictions of his art, but without a doubt, Nietzsche's vast knowledge and his almost flippant hard driving style combine to serve as a monumental explication of most of his philosophy.<br/><br/>Beyond Good and Evil is subti</span>
  <span id="freeText2653167053919338256" style="display:none">I have been hesitant to write this review simply because I have had so much fun re-reading this book, one which I consider, despite its small size, one of the most influential books on philosophy of the late 19th century. It is rare that one gets the chance to laugh at a philosopher's depictions of his art, but without a doubt, Nietzsche's vast knowledge and his almost flippant hard driving style combine to serve as a monumental explication of most of his philosophy.<br /><br />Beyond Good and Evil is subtitled <i> Prelude to a Philosophy of the Future </i> and, although I am embarrassed to say so, I (finally) understand how this is significant as well as perhaps accurate. It is easy to dismiss a great deal of what Nietzsche says as hyperbole, but on this reading, some years later, I stopped and spent time on each of his concepts. For the record, I think his remarks about women have been taken much too literally and the reaction to them seems far more hysterical (no pun intended) than what I consider a deeper meaning. Still, as others have said, perhaps he is taking some of that misogyny from Aristotle and perhaps Schopenhauer too. Of the three, Schopenhauer is the one who foams at the mouth, but I digress.<br /><br />While he uses a broad sword in his criticism, one must pay careful attention and it is difficult to find holes in his arguments, at least accepting his presuppositions: it is key that he is representing his views as art as much as he is writing them as philosophy, while he begins in the first few sections excoriating past philosophers for their dependence on dogmatic principles and the use of certain principles which he rejects out of hand. <br /><br />One of his first victims is the stoic principle of living according to nature which he essentially reduces to rubble in the center of the floor. It is a masterful job, arguing that it is the stoic who is assigning the value to what he calls nature around him and then following it circuitously. After he finishes with the stoics, he eviscerates pretty much every other philosophical field of thought, not without a certain degree of aplomb and, I think, humor.<br /><br />However when he talks about the Epicureans, it started me wondering exactly what he might have taken as Epicurean thought. Certainly he read Sextus Empiricus and Lucretius (and Cicero's criticisms, of course,) and some of the various fragments, but the major scrolls of Philodemus, a noted Roman Epicurean, were probably not published when Nietzsche was writing. I could not help but think myself constantly back with the Pre-Socratics and I tend to think that Nietzsche perhaps drew a great deal from some of them such as Democritus.<br /><br />The greatest difficulty with this book is how to abide with it: when he disembowels Kant for his categories and his antinomies, he hits the heart of what we are required to take as the book's essence. Nietzsche says, <blockquote> The question is how far an opinion is life-furthering, life-preserving, species-preserving, perhaps species-rearing, and we are fundamentally inclined to maintain that the falsest opinions (to which the synthetic judgments apriori belong) are the most indispensable to us, that without a recognition of logical fictions, without a comparison of reality with the purely imagined world of the absolute and immutable, without a constant counterfeiting of the world by means of numbers, man could not live---that the renunciation of false opinions would be a renunciation of life, a negation of life. TO RECOGNIZE UNTRUTH AS A CONDITION OF LIFE; that is certainly to impugn the traditional ideas of value in a dangerous manner, and a philosophy which ventures to do so, has thereby alone placed itself beyond good and evil. </blockquote><br /><br />Ultimately we must ask ourselves, first, whether Nietzsche was being prophetic, not in the sense of an abomination of his philosophy through people like Hitler (who often posed with his bust for pictures,)but through the growth in the denial of what we might term traditional values. What we have been witnessing, at least so it seems to me, for the last 100 years or more is not so much the negation of values, but the increasing negation of value, in and of itself. While it is true that if what he says is true... and his arguments are nothing short of well formed and literate, it remains whether one can truly persist and prosper simply through one's will to, not existence, but power. While cogent and tempting, one suspects that the atomic structure (in a figurative sense) of human psychology which tends to hold things in order, especially in such a field as ethical value negation, would probably cease to have any functional sense. Ultimately, then, even the negation of all values seems to be, sadly, circuitous. This is the very embodiment of the mythical Chinese dragon which consumes itself with necessity by chewing its tail.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2653167053919338256" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
